<div class="vector-body" id="bodyContent">
<div class="noprint" id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>

<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1033289096">.mw-parser-output .hatnote{font-style:italic}.mw-parser-output div.hatnote{padding-left:1.6em;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .hatnote i{font-style:normal}.mw-parser-output .hatnote+link+.hatnote{margin-top:-0.5em}</style><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">For other uses, see <a class="mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Climate_(disambiguation)" title="Climate (disambiguation)">Climate (disambiguation)</a>.</div>

<p><b>Climate</b> is the long-term <a href="/wiki/Weather" title="Weather">weather</a> pattern in an area, typically averaged over 30 years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IPCC-2021_1-0"><a href="#cite_note-IPCC-2021-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASA-20050201_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-NASA-20050201-2">[2]</a></sup> More rigorously, it is the mean and variability of <a href="/wiki/Meteorology" title="Meteorology">meteorological</a> variables over a time spanning from months to millions of years. Some of the <a href="/wiki/Meteorology" title="Meteorology">meteorological</a> variables that are commonly measured are <a href="/wiki/Temperature" title="Temperature">temperature</a>, <a href="/wiki/Humidity" title="Humidity">humidity</a>, <a href="/wiki/Atmospheric_pressure" title="Atmospheric pressure">atmospheric pressure</a>, <a href="/wiki/Wind" title="Wind">wind</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Precipitation" title="Precipitation">precipitation</a>. In a broader sense, climate is the state of the components of the <a href="/wiki/Climate_system" title="Climate system">climate system</a>, including the <a href="/wiki/Atmosphere" title="Atmosphere">atmosphere</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hydrosphere" title="Hydrosphere">hydrosphere</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cryosphere" title="Cryosphere">cryosphere</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lithosphere" title="Lithosphere">lithosphere</a> and <a href="/wiki/Biosphere" title="Biosphere">biosphere</a> and the interactions between them.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IPCC-2021_1-1"><a href="#cite_note-IPCC-2021-1">[1]</a></sup> The climate of a location is affected by its <a href="/wiki/Latitude" title="Latitude">latitude</a>/<a href="/wiki/Longitude" title="Longitude">longitude</a>, <a href="/wiki/Terrain" title="Terrain">terrain</a>, <a href="/wiki/Altitude" title="Altitude">altitude</a>, <a href="/wiki/Land_use" title="Land use">land use</a> and nearby <a href="/wiki/Body_of_water" title="Body of water">water bodies</a> and their currents.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup>
</p><p>Climates can be <a href="/wiki/Climate_classification" title="Climate classification">classified</a> according to the average and typical variables, most commonly <a href="/wiki/Temperature" title="Temperature">temperature</a> and <a href="/wiki/Precipitation" title="Precipitation">precipitation</a>. The most widely used classification scheme was the <a href="/wiki/K%C3%B6ppen_climate_classification" title="Köppen climate classification">Köppen climate classification</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Thornthwaite_climate_classification" title="Thornthwaite climate classification">Thornthwaite system</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup> in use since 1948, incorporates <a href="/wiki/Evapotranspiration" title="Evapotranspiration">evapotranspiration</a> along with temperature and <a href="/wiki/Precipitation" title="Precipitation">precipitation</a> information and is used in studying <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Biological_diversity" title="Biological diversity">biological diversity</a> and how <a href="/wiki/Climate_change" title="Climate change">climate change</a> affects it. Finally, the Bergeron and <a href="/wiki/Spatial_Synoptic_Classification_system" title="Spatial Synoptic Classification system">Spatial Synoptic Classification systems</a> focus on the origin of air masses that define the climate of a region.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Paleoclimatology" title="Paleoclimatology">Paleoclimatology</a> is the study of ancient climates. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Paleoclimatologists" title="Paleoclimatologists">Paleoclimatologists</a> seek to explain climate variations for all parts of the Earth during any given <a href="/wiki/Geology" title="Geology">geologic</a> period, beginning with the time of the Earth's formation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup> Since very few direct observations of climate were available before the 19th century, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Paleoclimate" title="Paleoclimate">paleoclimates</a> are inferred from <a href="/wiki/Proxy_(climate)" title="Proxy (climate)">proxy variables</a>. They include non-biotic evidence—such as sediments found in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lake_beds" title="Lake beds">lake beds</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ice_core" title="Ice core">ice cores</a>—and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Biotic_component" title="Biotic component">biotic</a> evidence—such as <a href="/wiki/Dendrochronology" title="Dendrochronology">tree rings</a> and coral. <a href="/wiki/Climate_model" title="Climate model">Climate models</a> are mathematical models of past, present, and future climates. Climate change may occur over long and short timescales from various factors. Recent warming is discussed in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Global_warming" title="Global warming">global warming</a>, which results in redistributions. For example, "a 3 °C [5 °F] change in mean annual temperature corresponds to a shift in isotherms of approximately 300–400 km [190–250 mi] in latitude (in the temperate zone) or 500 m [1,600 ft] in elevation. Therefore, species are expected to move upwards in elevation or towards the poles in <a href="/wiki/Latitude" title="Latitude">latitude</a> in response to shifting climate zones."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TIEE-20000201_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-TIEE-20000201-7">[7]</a></sup>
</p>
<div aria-labelledby="mw-toc-heading" class="toc" id="toc" role="navigation"><input class="toctogglecheckbox" id="toctogglecheckbox" role="button" style="display:none" type="checkbox"/><div class="toctitle" dir="ltr" lang="en"><h2 id="mw-toc-heading">Contents</h2><span class="toctogglespan"><label class="toctogglelabel" for="toctogglecheckbox"></label></span></div>

</ul>
</li>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Definition">Definition</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Climate&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Definition">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Climate (from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek_language" title="Ancient Greek language">Ancient Greek</a> <i> κλίμα</i> 'inclination') is commonly defined as the weather averaged over a long period.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup> The standard averaging period is 30 years,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup> but other periods may be used depending on the purpose. Climate also includes statistics other than the average, such as the magnitudes of day-to-day or year-to-year variations. The <a href="/wiki/Intergovernmental_Panel_on_Climate_Change" title="Intergovernmental Panel on Climate Change">Intergovernmental Panel on Climate Change</a> (IPCC) <a href="/wiki/IPCC_Third_Assessment_Report" title="IPCC Third Assessment Report">2001</a> glossary definition is as follows:
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r996844942">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 40px}.mw-parser-output .templatequote .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;padding-left:1.6em;margin-top:0}</style><blockquote class="templatequote"><p><i>Climate in a narrow sense is usually defined as the "average weather," or more rigorously, as the statistical description in terms of the mean and variability of relevant quantities over a period ranging from months to thousands or millions of years. The classical period is 30 years, as defined by the World Meteorological Organization (WMO). These quantities are most often surface variables such as temperature, precipitation, and wind. Climate in a wider sense is the state, including a statistical description, of the climate system.</i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup></p></blockquote>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/World_Meteorological_Organization" title="World Meteorological Organization">World Meteorological Organization</a> (WMO) describes "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Climate_normal" title="Climate normal">climate normals</a>" as "reference points used by <a href="/wiki/Climatology" title="Climatology">climatologists</a> to compare current climatological trends to that of the past or what is considered typical. A climate normal is defined as the arithmetic average of a climate element (e.g. temperature) over a 30-year period. A 30-year period is used as it is long enough to filter out any interannual variation or anomalies such as <a href="/wiki/El_Ni%C3%B1o%E2%80%93Southern_Oscillation" title="El Niño–Southern Oscillation">El Niño–Southern Oscillation</a>, but also short enough to be able to show longer climatic trends."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WMO_data_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-WMO_data-11">[11]</a></sup>
</p><p>The WMO originated from the <a href="/wiki/International_Meteorological_Organization" title="International Meteorological Organization">International Meteorological Organization</a> which set up a technical commission for climatology in 1929. At its 1934 <a href="/wiki/Wiesbaden" title="Wiesbaden">Wiesbaden</a> meeting the technical commission designated the thirty-year period from 1901 to 1930 as the reference time frame for climatological standard normals. In 1982 the WMO agreed to update climate normals, and these were subsequently completed on the basis of climate data from 1 January 1961 to 31 December 1990.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WMO_history_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-WMO_history-12">[12]</a></sup> The 1961-1990 climate normals serve as the baseline reference period. The next set of climate normals to be published by WMO is from 1991 to 2010.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup> Aside from collecting from the most common atmospheric variables (air temperature, pressure, precipitation and wind), other variables such as humidity, visibility, cloud amount, solar radiation, soil temperature, pan evaporation rate, days with thunder and days with hail are also collected to measure change in climate conditions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup>
</p><p>The difference between climate and weather is usefully summarized by the popular phrase "Climate is what you expect, weather is what you get."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup> Over <a href="/wiki/History" title="History">historical</a> time spans, there are a number of nearly constant variables that determine climate, including <a href="/wiki/Latitude" title="Latitude">latitude</a>, altitude, proportion of land to water, and proximity to oceans and mountains. All of these variables change only over periods of millions of years due to processes such as <a href="/wiki/Plate_tectonics" title="Plate tectonics">plate tectonics</a>. Other climate determinants are more dynamic: the <a href="/wiki/Thermohaline_circulation" title="Thermohaline circulation">thermohaline circulation</a> of the ocean leads to a 5 °C (41 °F) warming of the northern Atlantic Ocean compared to other ocean basins.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup> Other <a href="/wiki/Ocean_current" title="Ocean current">ocean currents</a> redistribute heat between land and water on a more regional scale. The density and type of vegetation coverage affects solar heat absorption,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup> water retention, and rainfall on a regional level. Alterations in the quantity of atmospheric <a href="/wiki/Greenhouse_gas" title="Greenhouse gas">greenhouse gases</a> determines the amount of solar energy retained by the planet, leading to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Global_warming" title="Global warming">global warming</a> or <a href="/wiki/Global_cooling" title="Global cooling">global cooling</a>. The variables which determine climate are numerous and the interactions complex, but there is general agreement that the broad outlines are understood, at least insofar as the determinants of historical climate change are concerned.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ledley1999_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ledley1999-18">[18]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Climate_classification">Climate classification<span class="anchor" id="Classification"></span></span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Climate&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Climate classification">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Climate_classification" title="Climate classification">Climate classification</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:K%C3%B6ppen-Geiger_Climate_Classification_Map_(1980%E2%80%932016)_no_borders.png"><img alt="Map of world dividing climate zones, largely influenced by latitude. The zones, going from the equator upward (and downward) are Tropical, Dry, Moderate, Continental and Polar. There are subzones within these zones." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2160" data-file-width="4320" decoding="async" height="110" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/32/K%C3%B6ppen-Geiger_Climate_Classification_Map_%281980%E2%80%932016%29_no_borders.png/220px-K%C3%B6ppen-Geiger_Climate_Classification_Map_%281980%E2%80%932016%29_no_borders.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/32/K%C3%B6ppen-Geiger_Climate_Classification_Map_%281980%E2%80%932016%29_no_borders.png/330px-K%C3%B6ppen-Geiger_Climate_Classification_Map_%281980%E2%80%932016%29_no_borders.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/32/K%C3%B6ppen-Geiger_Climate_Classification_Map_%281980%E2%80%932016%29_no_borders.png/440px-K%C3%B6ppen-Geiger_Climate_Classification_Map_%281980%E2%80%932016%29_no_borders.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:K%C3%B6ppen-Geiger_Climate_Classification_Map_(1980%E2%80%932016)_no_borders.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Worldwide <a href="/wiki/K%C3%B6ppen_climate_classification" title="Köppen climate classification">Köppen climate classifications</a></div></div></div>
<p>Climate classifications are systems that categorize the world's climates. A climate classification may correlate closely with a <a href="/wiki/Biome" title="Biome">biome</a> classification, as climate is a major influence on life in a region. One of the most used is the <a href="/wiki/K%C3%B6ppen_climate_classification" title="Köppen climate classification">Köppen climate classification</a> scheme first developed in 1899.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup>
</p><p>There are several ways to classify climates into similar regimes. Originally, <a href="/wiki/Clime" title="Clime">climes</a> were defined in <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greece" title="Ancient Greece">Ancient Greece</a> to describe the weather depending upon a location's latitude. Modern climate classification methods can be broadly divided into <i>genetic</i> methods, which focus on the causes of climate, and <i>empiric</i> methods, which focus on the effects of climate. Examples of genetic classification include methods based on the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Relative_frequency" title="Relative frequency">relative frequency</a> of different <a href="/wiki/Air_mass" title="Air mass">air mass</a> types or locations within <a href="/wiki/Synoptic_scale_meteorology" title="Synoptic scale meteorology">synoptic</a> weather disturbances. Examples of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Empirical" title="Empirical">empiric</a> classifications include <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Climate_zone" title="Climate zone">climate zones</a> defined by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Plant_hardiness" title="Plant hardiness">plant hardiness</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup> evapotranspiration,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-thorn_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-thorn-21">[21]</a></sup> or more generally the <a href="/wiki/K%C3%B6ppen_climate_classification" title="Köppen climate classification">Köppen climate classification</a> which was originally designed to identify the climates associated with certain <a href="/wiki/Biome" title="Biome">biomes</a>. A common shortcoming of these <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Classification_scheme" title="Classification scheme">classification schemes</a> is that they produce distinct boundaries between the zones they define, rather than the gradual transition of climate properties more common in nature.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Record">Record</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Climate&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Record">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Paleoclimatology">Paleoclimatology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Climate&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Paleoclimatology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Paleoclimatology" title="Paleoclimatology">Paleoclimatology</a></div>
<p>Paleoclimatology is the study of past climate over a great period of the <a href="/wiki/Earth" title="Earth">Earth</a>'s history. It uses evidence with different time scales (from decades to millennia) from ice sheets, tree rings, sediments, pollen, coral, and rocks to determine the past state of the climate. It demonstrates periods of stability and periods of change and can indicate whether changes follow patterns such as regular cycles.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Modern">Modern</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Climate&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Modern">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Instrumental_temperature_record" title="Instrumental temperature record">Instrumental temperature record</a> and <a href="/wiki/Satellite_temperature_measurements" title="Satellite temperature measurements">Satellite temperature measurements</a></div>
<p>Details of the modern climate record are known through the taking of measurements from such weather instruments as <a href="/wiki/Thermometer" title="Thermometer">thermometers</a>, <a href="/wiki/Barometer" title="Barometer">barometers</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Anemometer" title="Anemometer">anemometers</a> during the past few centuries. The instruments used to study weather over the modern time scale, their observation frequency, their known error, their immediate environment, and their exposure have changed over the years, which must be considered when studying the climate of centuries past.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup> Long-term modern climate records skew towards population centres and affluent countries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup> Since the 1960s, the launch of satellites allow records to be gathered on a global scale, including areas with little to no human presence, such as the Arctic region and oceans.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Climate_variability">Climate variability</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Climate&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Climate variability">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Climate_variability_and_change" title="Climate variability and change">Climate variability and change</a></div>
<p>Climate variability is the term to describe variations in the mean state and other characteristics of climate (such as chances or possibility of <a href="/wiki/Extreme_weather" title="Extreme weather">extreme weather</a>, etc.) "on all spatial and temporal scales beyond that of individual weather events."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEIPCC_AR5_WG1_Glossary20131451_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEIPCC_AR5_WG1_Glossary20131451-25">[25]</a></sup> Some of the variability does not appear to be caused systematically and occurs at random times. Such variability is called <i>random variability</i> or <i><a href="/wiki/Noise_(signal_processing)" title="Noise (signal processing)">noise</a></i>. On the other hand, periodic variability occurs relatively regularly and in distinct modes of variability or climate patterns.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERohliVega2018274_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERohliVega2018274-26">[26]</a></sup>
</p><p>There are close correlations between Earth's climate oscillations and astronomical factors (<a href="/wiki/Barycenter" title="Barycenter">barycenter</a> changes, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Solar_variation" title="Solar variation">solar variation</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cosmic_ray" title="Cosmic ray">cosmic ray</a> flux, <a href="/wiki/Cloud_albedo" title="Cloud albedo">cloud albedo</a> <a href="/wiki/Cloud_feedback" title="Cloud feedback">feedback</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Milankovic_cycle" title="Milankovic cycle">Milankovic cycles</a>), and modes of <a href="/wiki/Thermodynamics" title="Thermodynamics">heat distribution</a> between the ocean-atmosphere climate system. In some cases, current, historical and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Paleoclimate" title="Paleoclimate">paleoclimatological</a> natural oscillations may be masked by significant <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Volcanic_eruption" title="Volcanic eruption">volcanic eruptions</a>, <a href="/wiki/Impact_event" title="Impact event">impact events</a>, irregularities in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Climate_proxy" title="Climate proxy">climate proxy</a> data, <a href="/wiki/Positive_feedback" title="Positive feedback">positive feedback</a> processes or <a href="/wiki/Human_impact_on_the_environment" title="Human impact on the environment">anthropogenic</a> <a href="/wiki/Greenhouse_gas#Greenhouse_gas_emissions" title="Greenhouse gas">emissions</a> of substances such as <a href="/wiki/Greenhouse_gas" title="Greenhouse gas">greenhouse gases</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Scafetta_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-Scafetta-27">[27]</a></sup>
</p><p>Over the years, the definitions of <i>climate variability</i> and the related term <i><a href="/wiki/Climate_change" title="Climate change">climate change</a></i> have shifted. While the term <i>climate change</i> now implies change that is both long-term and of human causation, in the 1960s the word climate change was used for what we now describe as climate variability, that is, climatic inconsistencies and anomalies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERohliVega2018274_26-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERohliVega2018274-26">[26]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Climate_change">Climate change</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Climate&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Climate change">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Change_in_Average_Temperature_With_Fahrenheit.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="864" data-file-width="960" decoding="async" height="270" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/Change_in_Average_Temperature_With_Fahrenheit.svg/300px-Change_in_Average_Temperature_With_Fahrenheit.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/Change_in_Average_Temperature_With_Fahrenheit.svg/450px-Change_in_Average_Temperature_With_Fahrenheit.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/Change_in_Average_Temperature_With_Fahrenheit.svg/600px-Change_in_Average_Temperature_With_Fahrenheit.svg.png 2x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Change_in_Average_Temperature_With_Fahrenheit.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Average surface air temperatures from 2011 to 2021 compared to the 1956–1976 average. Source: <a href="/wiki/Goddard_Institute_for_Space_Studies" title="Goddard Institute for Space Studies">NASA</a></div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Global_Temperature_And_Forces_With_Fahrenheit.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="720" data-file-width="960" decoding="async" height="225" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/08/Global_Temperature_And_Forces_With_Fahrenheit.svg/300px-Global_Temperature_And_Forces_With_Fahrenheit.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/08/Global_Temperature_And_Forces_With_Fahrenheit.svg/450px-Global_Temperature_And_Forces_With_Fahrenheit.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/08/Global_Temperature_And_Forces_With_Fahrenheit.svg/600px-Global_Temperature_And_Forces_With_Fahrenheit.svg.png 2x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Global_Temperature_And_Forces_With_Fahrenheit.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Observed temperature from NASA<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nasa_temperatures_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-nasa_temperatures-28">[28]</a></sup> vs the 1850–1900 average used by the IPCC as a pre-industrial baseline.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ipcc_pre_industrial_baseline_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-ipcc_pre_industrial_baseline-29">[29]</a></sup> The primary driver for increased global temperatures in the industrial era is human activity, with natural forces adding variability.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-USGCRP_Chapter_3_Figure_3-1_panel_2_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-USGCRP_Chapter_3_Figure_3-1_panel_2-30">[30]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Climate_change" title="Climate change">Climate change</a></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Global_temperature_record" title="Global temperature record">Global temperature record</a>, <a href="/wiki/List_of_weather_records" title="List of weather records">List of weather records</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Attribution_of_recent_climate_change" title="Attribution of recent climate change">Attribution of recent climate change</a></div>
<p>Climate change is the variation in global or regional climates over time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup> It reflects changes in the variability or average state of the atmosphere over time scales ranging from decades to millions of years. These changes can be caused by processes internal to the <a href="/wiki/Earth" title="Earth">Earth</a>, external forces (e.g. variations in sunlight intensity) or, more recently, human activities.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYT-20151128-jg_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-NYT-20151128-jg-33">[33]</a></sup>
In recent usage, especially in the context of <a href="/wiki/Environmental_policy" title="Environmental policy">environmental policy</a>, the term "climate change" often refers only to changes in modern climate, including the rise in average surface <a href="/wiki/Temperature" title="Temperature">temperature</a> known as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Global_warming" title="Global warming">global warming</a>. In some cases, the term is also used with a presumption of human causation, as in the <a href="/wiki/United_Nations" title="United Nations">United Nations</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/UNFCCC" title="UNFCCC">Framework Convention on Climate Change</a> (UNFCCC). The UNFCCC uses "climate variability" for non-human caused variations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup>
</p><p>Earth has undergone periodic climate shifts in the past, including four major <a href="/wiki/Ice_age" title="Ice age">ice ages</a>. These consisting of glacial periods where conditions are colder than normal, separated by <a href="/wiki/Interglacial" title="Interglacial">interglacial</a> periods. The accumulation of snow and ice during a glacial period increases the surface <a href="/wiki/Albedo" title="Albedo">albedo</a>, reflecting more of the Sun's energy into space and maintaining a lower atmospheric temperature. Increases in <a href="/wiki/Greenhouse_gas" title="Greenhouse gas">greenhouse gases</a>, such as by <a href="/wiki/Volcanic_impacts_on_the_oceans" title="Volcanic impacts on the oceans">volcanic activity</a>, can increase the global temperature and produce an interglacial period. Suggested causes of ice age periods include the positions of the <a href="/wiki/Continent" title="Continent">continents</a>, variations in the Earth's orbit, changes in the solar output, and volcanism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup> However, these naturally-caused changes in climate occur on a much slower time scale than the present rate of change which is caused by the emission of greenhouse gases by human activities.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Climate_models">Climate models</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Climate&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Climate models">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p><a href="/wiki/Climate_model" title="Climate model">Climate models</a> use quantitative methods to simulate the interactions and transfer of radiative energy between the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Earth%27s_atmosphere" title="Earth's atmosphere">atmosphere</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Ocean" title="Ocean">oceans</a>, land surface and ice through a series of physics equations. They are used for a variety of purposes; from the study of the dynamics of the weather and climate system, to projections of future climate. All climate models balance, or very nearly balance, incoming energy as short wave (including visible) electromagnetic radiation to the Earth with outgoing energy as long wave (infrared) electromagnetic radiation from the earth. Any imbalance results in a change in the average temperature of the earth.
</p><p>Climate models are available on different resolutions ranging from &gt;100 km to 1 km. High resolutions in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Global_climate_model" title="Global climate model">global climate models</a> are computational very demanding and only few global datasets exists. Global climate models can be dynamically or statistically downscaled to regional climate models to analyze impacts of climate change on a local scale. Examples are ICON<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup> or mechanistically downscaled data such as CHELSA (Climatologies at high resolution for the earth's land surface areas).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup>
</p><p>The most talked-about applications of these models in recent years have been their use to infer the consequences of increasing greenhouse gases in the atmosphere, primarily <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide" title="Carbon dioxide">carbon dioxide</a> (see <a href="/wiki/Greenhouse_gas" title="Greenhouse gas">greenhouse gas</a>). These models predict an upward trend in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Surface_temperature_record" title="Surface temperature record">global mean surface temperature</a>, with the most rapid increase in temperature being projected for the higher latitudes of the Northern Hemisphere.
</p><p>Models can range from relatively simple to quite complex:
</p>
<ul><li>Simple radiant heat transfer model that treats the earth as a single point and averages outgoing energy</li>
<li>this can be expanded vertically (radiative-convective models), or horizontally</li>
<li>finally, (coupled) atmosphere–ocean–<a href="/wiki/Sea_ice" title="Sea ice">sea ice</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Global_climate_model" title="Global climate model">global climate models</a> discretise and solve the full equations for mass and energy transfer and radiant exchange.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup></li></ul>

<li id="cite_note-8"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-8">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation encyclopaedia cs1"><a class="external text" href="http://amsglossary.allenpress.com/glossary/search?id=climate1" rel="nofollow">"Climate"</a>. <i>Glossary of Meteorology</i>. <a href="/wiki/American_Meteorological_Society" title="American Meteorological Society">American Meteorological Society</a>. <a class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20110707113544/http://amsglossary.allenpress.com/glossary/search?id=climate1" rel="nofollow">Archived</a> from the original on 2011-07-07<span class="reference-accessdate">.
